          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           <a href="/wiki/Hokusai" title="Hokusai">Hokusai</a>'s 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Ukiyo-e" title="Ukiyo-e">ukiyo-e</a> <a href="/wiki/Woodblock_printing_in_Japan" title="Woodblock printing in Japan">woodblock print</a> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Wave_off_Kanagawa" title="The Great Wave off Kanagawa">The Great Wave off Kanagawa</a></i>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="tsingle" style="width:157px;max-width:157px">
          <div class="thumbimage" style="height:103px;overflow:hidden">
           <a href="/wiki/File:150504_Ritsurin_Park_Takamatsu_Kagawa_pref_Japan01s3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/150504_Ritsurin_Park_Takamatsu_Kagawa_pref_Japan01s3.jpg/155px-150504_Ritsurin_Park_Takamatsu_Kagawa_pref_Japan01s3.jpg" decoding="async" width="155" height="103" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/150504_Ritsurin_Park_Takamatsu_Kagawa_pref_Japan01s3.jpg/233px-150504_Ritsurin_Park_Takamatsu_Kagawa_pref_Japan01s3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/150504_Ritsurin_Park_Takamatsu_Kagawa_pref_Japan01s3.jpg/310px-150504_Ritsurin_Park_Takamatsu_Kagawa_pref_Japan01s3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6000" data-file-height="4000"></a>
          </div>
          <div class="thumbcaption">
           <a href="/wiki/Ritsurin_Garden" title="Ritsurin Garden">Ritsurin Garden</a>, one of the most famous strolling gardens in Japan
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The history of <a href="/wiki/Japanese_painting" title="Japanese painting">Japanese painting</a> exhibits synthesis and competition between native <a href="/wiki/Japanese_esthetics" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese esthetics">Japanese esthetics</a> and imported ideas.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-261">[260]</a></sup> The interaction between Japanese and European art has been significant: for example <a href="/wiki/Ukiyo-e" title="Ukiyo-e">ukiyo-e</a> prints, which began to be exported in the 19th century in the movement known as <a href="/wiki/Japonism" class="mw-redirect" title="Japonism">Japonism</a>, had a significant influence on the development of modern art in the West, most notably on <a href="/wiki/Post-Impressionism" title="Post-Impressionism">post-Impressionism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_261-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-261">[260]</a></sup> Japanese <a href="/wiki/Manga" title="Manga">manga</a> developed in the 20th century and have become popular worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-Kinko_Ito_456–475_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kinko_Ito_456–475-262">[261]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Japanese_architecture" title="Japanese architecture">Japanese architecture</a> is a combination between local and other influences. It has traditionally been typified by wooden or mud plaster structures, elevated slightly off the ground, with tiled or thatched roofs.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">[262]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ise_Grand_Shrine" title="Ise Grand Shrine">Shrines of Ise</a> have been celebrated as the prototype of Japanese architecture.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">[263]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Minka" title="Minka">Traditional housing</a> and many <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Buddhist_architecture" title="Japanese Buddhist architecture">temple buildings</a> see the use of <a href="/wiki/Tatami" title="Tatami">tatami</a> mats and <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dji" class="mw-redirect" title="Shōji">sliding doors</a> that break down the distinction between rooms and indoor and outdoor space.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">[264]</a></sup> Since the 19th century, Japan has incorporated much of Western <a href="/wiki/Modern_architecture" title="Modern architecture">modern architecture</a> into construction and design.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">[265]</a></sup> It was not until after World War II that Japanese architects made an impression on the international scene, firstly with the work of architects like <a href="/wiki/Kenz%C5%8D_Tange" title="Kenzō Tange">Kenzō Tange</a> and then with movements like <a href="/wiki/Metabolist_Movement" class="mw-redirect" title="Metabolist Movement">Metabolism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">[266]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature_and_philosophy">Literature and philosophy</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_literature" title="Japanese literature">Japanese literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Japanese_poetry" title="Japanese poetry">Japanese poetry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_philosophy" title="Japanese philosophy">Japanese philosophy</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg/220px-Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="103" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg/330px-Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg/440px-Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2142" data-file-height="1000"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Genji_emaki_01003_001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>12th-century <a href="/wiki/Genji_Monogatari_Emaki" title="Genji Monogatari Emaki">illustrated handscroll</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Genji" title="The Tale of Genji">The Tale of Genji</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/National_Treasures_of_Japan" class="mw-redirect" title="National Treasures of Japan">National Treasure</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The earliest works of Japanese literature include the <i><a href="/wiki/Kojiki" title="Kojiki">Kojiki</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Nihon_Shoki" title="Nihon Shoki">Nihon Shoki</a></i> chronicles and the <i><a href="/wiki/Man%27y%C5%8Dsh%C5%AB" title="Man'yōshū">Man'yōshū</a></i> <a href="/wiki/List_of_Japanese_poetry_anthologies" title="List of Japanese poetry anthologies">poetry anthology</a>, all from the 8th century and written in Chinese characters.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">[267]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">[268]</a></sup> In the early Heian period, the system of <a href="/wiki/Phonogram_(linguistics)" title="Phonogram (linguistics)">phonograms</a> known as <i>kana</i> (<a href="/wiki/Hiragana" title="Hiragana">hiragana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Katakana" title="Katakana">katakana</a>) was developed.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">[269]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_the_Bamboo_Cutter" title="The Tale of the Bamboo Cutter">The Tale of the Bamboo Cutter</a></i> is considered the oldest extant Japanese narrative.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">[270]</a></sup> An account of court life is given in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Pillow_Book" title="The Pillow Book">The Pillow Book</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Sei_Sh%C5%8Dnagon" title="Sei Shōnagon">Sei Shōnagon</a>, while <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tale_of_Genji" title="The Tale of Genji">The Tale of Genji</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Murasaki_Shikibu" title="Murasaki Shikibu">Murasaki Shikibu</a> is often described as the world's first novel.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">[271]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">[272]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>During the Edo period, the <a href="/wiki/Ch%C5%8Dnin" title="Chōnin">chōnin</a> ("townspeople") overtook the samurai aristocracy as producers and consumers of literature. The popularity of the works of <a href="/wiki/Saikaku" class="mw-redirect" title="Saikaku">Saikaku</a>, for example, reveals this change in readership and authorship, while <a href="/wiki/Matsuo_Bash%C5%8D" title="Matsuo Bashō">Bashō</a> revivified the poetic tradition of the <a href="/wiki/Kokinsh%C5%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Kokinshū">Kokinshū</a> with his <a href="/wiki/Haikai" title="Haikai">haikai</a> (<a href="/wiki/Haiku" title="Haiku">haiku</a>) and wrote the poetic travelogue <i><a href="/wiki/Oku_no_Hosomichi" title="Oku no Hosomichi">Oku no Hosomichi</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">[273]</a></sup> The Meiji era saw the decline of traditional literary forms as Japanese literature integrated Western influences. <a href="/wiki/Natsume_S%C5%8Dseki" title="Natsume Sōseki">Natsume Sōseki</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mori_%C5%8Cgai" title="Mori Ōgai">Mori Ōgai</a> were significant novelists in the early 20th century, followed by <a href="/wiki/Ry%C5%ABnosuke_Akutagawa" title="Ryūnosuke Akutagawa">Ryūnosuke Akutagawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jun%27ichir%C5%8D_Tanizaki" title="Jun'ichirō Tanizaki">Jun'ichirō Tanizaki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kaf%C5%AB_Nagai" title="Kafū Nagai">Kafū Nagai</a> and, more recently, <a href="/wiki/Haruki_Murakami" title="Haruki Murakami">Haruki Murakami</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kenji_Nakagami" title="Kenji Nakagami">Kenji Nakagami</a>. Japan has two <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize-winning</a> authors&nbsp;– <a href="/wiki/Yasunari_Kawabata" title="Yasunari Kawabata">Yasunari Kawabata</a> (1968) and <a href="/wiki/Kenzabur%C5%8D_%C5%8Ce" title="Kenzaburō Ōe">Kenzaburō Ōe</a> (1994).<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">[274]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Japanese philosophy has historically been a <a href="/wiki/Information_fusion" class="mw-redirect" title="Information fusion">fusion</a> of both foreign, particularly <a href="/wiki/Chinese_philosophy" title="Chinese philosophy">Chinese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">Western</a>, and uniquely Japanese elements. In its literary forms, Japanese philosophy began about fourteen centuries ago. Confucian ideals remain evident in the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_society" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese society">Japanese concept of society</a> and the self, and in the organization of the government and the structure of society.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-276">[275]</a></sup> Buddhism has profoundly impacted Japanese psychology, metaphysics, and esthetics.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">[276]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Performing_arts">Performing arts</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Japan" title="Music of Japan">Music of Japan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theatre_of_Japan" title="Theatre of Japan">Theatre of Japan</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:%E6%98%A5%E6%97%A5%E7%A5%9E%E7%A4%BE%E3%83%BC%E7%AF%A0%E5%B1%B1%E3%83%BC%E7%BF%81%E5%A5%89%E7%B4%8DP1011774.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/%E6%98%A5%E6%97%A5%E7%A5%9E%E7%A4%BE%E3%83%BC%E7%AF%A0%E5%B1%B1%E3%83%BC%E7%BF%81%E5%A5%89%E7%B4%8DP1011774.jpg/220px-%E6%98%A5%E6%97%A5%E7%A5%9E%E7%A4%BE%E3%83%BC%E7%AF%A0%E5%B1%B1%E3%83%BC%E7%BF%81%E5%A5%89%E7%B4%8DP1011774.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/%E6%98%A5%E6%97%A5%E7%A5%9E%E7%A4%BE%E3%83%BC%E7%AF%A0%E5%B1%B1%E3%83%BC%E7%BF%81%E5%A5%89%E7%B4%8DP1011774.jpg/330px-%E6%98%A5%E6%97%A5%E7%A5%9E%E7%A4%BE%E3%83%BC%E7%AF%A0%E5%B1%B1%E3%83%BC%E7%BF%81%E5%A5%89%E7%B4%8DP1011774.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/%E6%98%A5%E6%97%A5%E7%A5%9E%E7%A4%BE%E3%83%BC%E7%AF%A0%E5%B1%B1%E3%83%BC%E7%BF%81%E5%A5%89%E7%B4%8DP1011774.jpg/440px-%E6%98%A5%E6%97%A5%E7%A5%9E%E7%A4%BE%E3%83%BC%E7%AF%A0%E5%B1%B1%E3%83%BC%E7%BF%81%E5%A5%89%E7%B4%8DP1011774.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:%E6%98%A5%E6%97%A5%E7%A5%9E%E7%A4%BE%E3%83%BC%E7%AF%A0%E5%B1%B1%E3%83%BC%E7%BF%81%E5%A5%89%E7%B4%8DP1011774.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><i><a href="/wiki/Noh" title="Noh">Noh</a></i> performance at a Shinto shrine
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Japanese music is eclectic and diverse. Many <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Japanese_musical_instruments" title="Traditional Japanese musical instruments">instruments</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Koto_(instrument)" title="Koto (instrument)">koto</a>, were introduced in the 9th and 10th centuries. The popular <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Japan#Folk_music" title="Music of Japan">folk music</a>, with the guitar-like <a href="/wiki/Shamisen" title="Shamisen">shamisen</a>, dates from the 16th century.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">[277]</a></sup> Western classical music, introduced in the late 19th century, forms an integral part of Japanese culture.<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">[278]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Taiko#Kumi-daiko" title="Taiko">Kumi-daiko</a> (ensemble drumming) was developed in postwar Japan and became very popular in North America.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">[279]</a></sup> Popular music in post-war Japan has been heavily influenced by American and European trends, which has led to the evolution of <a href="/wiki/J-pop" title="J-pop">J-pop</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">[280]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Karaoke" title="Karaoke">Karaoke</a> is a significant cultural activity.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">[281]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The four traditional theaters from Japan are <i><a href="/wiki/Noh" title="Noh">noh</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Ky%C5%8Dgen" title="Kyōgen">kyōgen</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Kabuki" title="Kabuki">kabuki</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Bunraku" title="Bunraku">bunraku</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">[282]</a></sup> Noh is one of the oldest continuous theater traditions in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">[283]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Customs_and_holidays">Customs and holidays</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Etiquette_in_Japan" title="Etiquette in Japan">Etiquette in Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Public_holidays_in_Japan" title="Public holidays in Japan">Public holidays in Japan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_festivals" title="Japanese festivals">Japanese festivals</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg/220px-Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg/330px-Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg/440px-Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg 2x" data-file-width="674" data-file-height="412"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Young_ladies_at_Harajuku.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Young ladies celebrate <a href="/wiki/Coming_of_Age_Day" title="Coming of Age Day">Coming of Age Day</a><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span lang="ja" title="Japanese-language text">成人の日</span>, <i lang="ja-Latn" title="Hepburn transliteration">Seijin no Hi</i>)</span> in <a href="/wiki/Harajuku" title="Harajuku">Harajuku</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/Ishin-denshin" title="Ishin-denshin">Ishin-denshin</a><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span lang="ja" title="Japanese-language text">以心伝心</span>)</span> is a Japanese idiom which denotes a form of interpersonal communication through unspoken mutual understanding.<sup id="cite_ref-Maynard-1993_285-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maynard-1993-285">[284]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Isagiyosa" title="Isagiyosa">Isagiyosa</a><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span lang="ja" title="Japanese-language text">潔さ</span>)</span> is a virtue of the capability of accepting death with composure. <a href="/wiki/Cherry_blossom" title="Cherry blossom">Cherry blossoms</a> are a symbol of isagiyosa in the sense of embracing the transience of the world.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">[285]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hansei" title="Hansei">Hansei</a><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span lang="ja" title="Japanese-language text">反省</span>)</span> is a central idea in Japanese culture, meaning to acknowledge one's own mistake and to pledge improvement. <a href="/wiki/Kotodama" title="Kotodama">Kotodama</a><span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span lang="ja" title="Japanese-language text">言霊</span>)</span> refers to the Japanese belief that mystical powers dwell in words and names.<sup id="cite_ref-face-saving_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-face-saving-287">[286]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Officially, Japan has 16 national, government-recognized holidays. Public holidays in Japan are regulated by the Public Holiday Law<span style="font-weight: normal"> (<span lang="ja" title="Japanese-language text">国民の祝日に関する法律</span>, <i lang="ja-Latn" title="Hepburn transliteration">Kokumin no Shukujitsu ni Kansuru Hōritsu</i>)</span> of 1948.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">[287]</a></sup> Beginning in 2000, Japan implemented the <a href="/wiki/Happy_Monday_System" title="Happy Monday System">Happy Monday System</a>, which moved a number of national holidays to Monday in order to obtain a long weekend.<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">[288]</a></sup> The national holidays in Japan are <a href="/wiki/Japanese_New_Year" title="Japanese New Year">New Year's Day</a> on January 1, <a href="/wiki/Coming_of_Age_Day" title="Coming of Age Day">Coming of Age Day</a> on the second Monday of January, <a href="/wiki/National_Foundation_Day" title="National Foundation Day">National Foundation Day</a> on February 11, <a href="/wiki/The_Emperor%27s_Birthday" title="The Emperor's Birthday">The Emperor's Birthday</a> on February 23, <a href="/wiki/Vernal_Equinox_Day" title="Vernal Equinox Day">Vernal Equinox Day</a> on March 20 or 21, <a href="/wiki/Sh%C5%8Dwa_Day" title="Shōwa Day">Shōwa Day</a> on April 29, <a href="/wiki/Constitution_Memorial_Day" title="Constitution Memorial Day">Constitution Memorial Day</a> on May 3, <a href="/wiki/Greenery_Day" title="Greenery Day">Greenery Day</a> on May 4, <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_Day_(Japan)" title="Children's Day (Japan)">Children's Day</a> on May 5, <a href="/wiki/Marine_Day" title="Marine Day">Marine Day</a> on the third Monday of July, <a href="/wiki/Mountain_Day" title="Mountain Day">Mountain Day</a> on August 11, <a href="/wiki/Respect_for_the_Aged_Day" title="Respect for the Aged Day">Respect for the Aged Day</a> on the third Monday of September, <a href="/wiki/September_equinox" title="September equinox">Autumnal Equinox</a> on September 23 or 24, <a href="/wiki/Health_and_Sports_Day" title="Health and Sports Day">Health and Sports Day</a> on the second Monday of October, <a href="/wiki/Culture_Day" title="Culture Day">Culture Day</a> on November 3, and <a href="/wiki/Labor_Thanksgiving_Day" title="Labor Thanksgiving Day">Labor Thanksgiving Day</a> on November 23.<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">[289]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">Cuisine</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Japanese_cuisine" title="Japanese cuisine">Japanese cuisine</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:East_West_sushi_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/East_West_sushi_01.jpg/220px-East_West_sushi_01.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/East_West_sushi_01.jpg/330px-East_West_sushi_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/East_West_sushi_01.jpg/440px-East_West_sushi_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6798" data-file-height="4492"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:East_West_sushi_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>A plate of <i><a href="/wiki/Sushi" title="Sushi">nigiri-zushi</a></i>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Japanese cuisine offers a vast array of <a href="/wiki/Japanese_regional_cuisine" title="Japanese regional cuisine">regional specialties</a> that use traditional recipes and local ingredients.<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">[290]</a></sup> Seafood and <a href="/wiki/Japanese_rice" title="Japanese rice">Japanese rice</a> or <a href="/wiki/Japanese_noodles" title="Japanese noodles">noodles</a> are traditional staples.<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">[291]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Japanese_curry" title="Japanese curry">Japanese curry</a>, since its introduction to Japan from <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British India</a>, is so widely consumed that it can be termed a <a href="/wiki/National_dish" title="National dish">national dish</a>, alongside <a href="/wiki/Ramen" title="Ramen">ramen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sushi" title="Sushi">sushi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">[292]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">[293]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">[294]</a></sup> Traditional Japanese sweets are known as <i>wagashi</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Goldstein2015_296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Goldstein2015-296">[295]</a></sup> Ingredients such as <a href="/wiki/Red_bean_paste" title="Red bean paste">red bean paste</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mochi" title="Mochi">mochi</a> are used. More modern-day tastes includes <a href="/wiki/Green_tea_ice_cream" title="Green tea ice cream">green tea ice cream</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FujitaStallings2008_297-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FujitaStallings2008-297">[296]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Popular Japanese beverages include <a href="/wiki/Sake" title="Sake">sake</a>, which is a brewed rice beverage that typically contains 14–17% alcohol and is made by multiple fermentation of rice.<sup id="cite_ref-Batt2014_298-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Batt2014-298">[297]</a></sup> Beer has been brewed in Japan since the late 17th century.<sup id="cite_ref-BoultonQuain2013_299-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BoultonQuain2013-299">[298]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Green_tea" title="Green tea">Green tea</a> is produced in Japan and prepared in forms such as <a href="/wiki/Matcha" title="Matcha">matcha</a>, used in the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_tea_ceremony" title="Japanese tea ceremony">Japanese tea ceremony</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">[299]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cinema_of_Japan" title="Cinema of Japan">Cinema of Japan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">Anime</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Media_of_Japan" class="mw-redirect" title="Media of Japan">Media of Japan</a>
      </div> 
      <p>According to the 2015 NHK survey on television viewing in Japan, 79 percent of Japanese watch television daily.<sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">[300]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Japanese_television_drama" title="Japanese television drama">Japanese television dramas</a> are viewed both within Japan and internationally;<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">[301]</a></sup> other popular shows are in the genres of <a href="/wiki/Japanese_variety_show" title="Japanese variety show">variety shows</a>, comedy, and news programs.<sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">[302]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Japanese_newspapers" title="Japanese newspapers">Japanese newspapers</a> are among the most circulated in the world as of 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Japan&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">[303]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Japan has one of the oldest and largest film industries globally.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">[304]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ishir%C5%8D_Honda" title="Ishirō Honda">Ishirō Honda</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Godzilla_(1954_film)" title="Godzilla (1954 film)">Godzilla</a></i> became an international icon of Japan and spawned an entire subgenre of <i><a href="/wiki/Kaiju" title="Kaiju">kaiju</a></i> films, as well as the longest-running film franchise in history.<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">[305]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">[306]</a></sup> Japanese animated films and television series, known as <a href="/wiki/Anime" title="Anime">anime</a>, were largely influenced by Japanese manga and have been extensively popular in the West. Japan is a world-renowned powerhouse of animation.<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">[307]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">[308]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sport_in_Japan" title="Sport in Japan">Sport in Japan</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Aki_basho_dohy%C5%8D-iri_on_Sept._28_2014.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Aki_basho_dohy%C5%8D-iri_on_Sept._28_2014.jpg/220px-Aki_basho_dohy%C5%8D-iri_on_Sept._28_2014.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Aki_basho_dohy%C5%8D-iri_on_Sept._28_2014.jpg/330px-Aki_basho_dohy%C5%8D-iri_on_Sept._28_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Aki_basho_dohy%C5%8D-iri_on_Sept._28_2014.jpg/440px-Aki_basho_dohy%C5%8D-iri_on_Sept._28_2014.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6016" data-file-height="4016"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Aki_basho_dohy%C5%8D-iri_on_Sept._28_2014.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Sumo" title="Sumo">Sumo</a> wrestlers form around the referee during the ring-entering ceremony
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Traditionally, <a href="/wiki/Sumo" title="Sumo">sumo</a> is considered Japan's national sport.<sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310">[309]</a></sup> Japanese martial arts such as <a href="/wiki/Judo" title="Judo">judo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kendo" title="Kendo">kendo</a> are taught as part of the compulsory junior high school curriculum.<sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">[310]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Baseball_in_Japan" title="Baseball in Japan">Baseball</a> is the most popular spectator sport in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">[311]</a></sup> Japan's top professional league, <a href="/wiki/Nippon_Professional_Baseball" title="Nippon Professional Baseball">Nippon Professional Baseball</a>, was established in 1936.<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">[312]</a></sup> Since the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/J._League" class="mw-redirect" title="J. League">Japan Professional Football League</a> in 1992, association football has gained a wide following.<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">[313]</a></sup> The country co-hosted the <a href="/wiki/2002_FIFA_World_Cup" title="2002 FIFA World Cup">2002 FIFA World Cup</a> with South Korea.<sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">[314]</a></sup> Japan has one of the most successful football teams in Asia, winning the <a href="/wiki/AFC_Asian_Cup" title="AFC Asian Cup">Asian Cup</a> four times,<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">[315]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/2011_FIFA_Women%27s_World_Cup" title="2011 FIFA Women's World Cup">FIFA Women's World Cup</a> in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">[316]</a></sup> Golf is also popular in Japan.<sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">[317]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In <a href="/wiki/Motorsport" title="Motorsport">motorsport</a>, Japanese automotive manufacturers have been successful in multiple different categories, with titles and victories in series such as <a href="/wiki/Formula_One" title="Formula One">Formula One</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grand_Prix_motorcycle_racing" title="Grand Prix motorcycle racing">MotoGP</a>, <a href="/wiki/IndyCar_Series" title="IndyCar Series">IndyCar</a>, <a href="/wiki/World_Rally_Championship" title="World Rally Championship">World Rally Championship</a>, <a href="/wiki/FIA_World_Endurance_Championship" title="FIA World Endurance Championship">World Endurance Championship</a>, <a href="/wiki/World_Touring_Car_Championship" title="World Touring Car Championship">World Touring Car Championship</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Touring_Car_Championship" title="British Touring Car Championship">British Touring Car Championship</a> and the <a href="/wiki/WeatherTech_SportsCar_Championship" title="WeatherTech SportsCar Championship">IMSA SportsCar Championship</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">[318]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">[319]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">[320]</a></sup> Three Japanese drivers have achieved podium finishes in Formula One, and drivers from Japan have victories at the <a href="/wiki/Indianapolis_500" title="Indianapolis 500">Indianapolis 500</a> and the <a href="/wiki/24_Hours_of_Le_Mans" title="24 Hours of Le Mans">24 Hours of Le Mans</a>, in addition to success in domestic championships.<sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">[321]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">[322]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Super_GT" title="Super GT">Super GT</a> is the most popular national series in Japan, while <a href="/wiki/Super_Formula_Championship" title="Super Formula Championship">Super Formula</a> is the top level domestic open-wheel series.<sup id="cite_ref-324" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-324">[323]</a></sup> The country hosts major races such as the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Grand_Prix" title="Japanese Grand Prix">Japanese Grand Prix</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">[324]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Japan hosted the Summer Olympics in <a href="/wiki/1964_Summer_Olympics" title="1964 Summer Olympics">Tokyo in 1964</a> and the Winter Olympics in <a href="/wiki/1972_Winter_Olympics" title="1972 Winter Olympics">Sapporo in 1972</a> and <a href="/wiki/1998_Winter_Olympics" title="1998 Winter Olympics">Nagano in 1998</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">[325]</a></sup> The country hosted the official <a href="/wiki/2006_Basketball_World_Championship" class="mw-redirect" title="2006 Basketball World Championship">2006 Basketball World Championship</a><sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">[326]</a></sup> and will co-host the <a href="/wiki/2023_Basketball_World_Championship" class="mw-redirect" title="2023 Basketball World Championship">2023 Basketball World Championship</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">[327]</a></sup> Tokyo will host the <a href="/wiki/2020_Summer_Olympics" title="2020 Summer Olympics">2020 Summer Olympics</a>, making Tokyo the first Asian city to host the Olympics twice.<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">[328]</a></sup> The country gained the hosting rights for the official <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Volleyball_World_Championship" class="mw-redirect" title="Women's Volleyball World Championship">Women's Volleyball World Championship</a> on five occasions, more than any other nation.<sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">[329]</a></sup> Japan is the most successful Asian <a href="/wiki/Rugby_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Rugby Union">Rugby Union</a> country<sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331">[330]</a></sup> and hosted the 2019 IRB <a href="/wiki/Rugby_World_Cup" title="Rugby World Cup">Rugby World Cup</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-332" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-332">[331]</a></sup> </p>

      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2> 
      <div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;"> 
       <div class="mw-references-wrap">
        <ol class="references"> 
         <li id="cite_note-fn1-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-fn1_2-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In English, the official name of the country is simply "Japan".<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> In Japanese, the name of the country as it appears on official documents, including the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Japan" title="Constitution of Japan">country's constitution</a>, is <span lang="ja" title="Japanese-language text">日本国</span> ( <span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ja-nippon_nihonkoku.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20"></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/86/Ja-nippon_nihonkoku.ogg" class="internal" title="Ja-nippon nihonkoku.ogg"><i>Nippon-koku</i></a></span></span> or <i>Nihon-koku</i>), meaning "State of Japan". Despite this, the short-form name 日本 (<i>Nippon</i> or <i>Nihon</i>) is often used officially.</span> </li> 
        </ol>
       </div>
      </div>
